Refutation of Three Christian Interpretations of the Union
Sanusi critiques three Christian interpretations of the union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's critique of three Christian interpretations of the union of the Word with Jesus, and why he deems their doctrine unintelligible.

Core Idea
Three absurd interpretations expose the incoherence of the Christian hypostatic union.

Sanusi examines the Christian claim that the divine Word united with Jesus' human nature. He presents three interpretations offered by Christians themselves and demonstrates each one's logical impossibility. This passage shows how aqeedah defends Islamic monotheism by refuting rival doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Sanusi's argument.
Notice the structure: claim, three interpretations, conclusion.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Find the three interpretations listed: (1) subsistence as accident in substance, (2) mixing and blending, (3) impression. Note how Sanusi refutes each.
Each refutation uses a different logical flaw.
3
Reflect on why each fails
Consider why each interpretation is impossible according to Sanusi. For example, the first requires separation from the divine essence, which Christians deny. The second mixes physical with non-physical. The third produces only a likeness, not a true union.
The overall conclusion is that the Christian doctrine is unintelligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم زعموا أيضاً : أنَّ أقنوم العلم منها — ويُسمَّى : الكلمة - اتحد بناسوت عيسى ؛ أي : جسدِهِ ، ومِنْ ثَمَّ كَانَ إلها عندهم .
واختلفوا في معنى اتحادِ الكلمة بهِ :
فمنهم مَنْ فسَّرَ قيامَ الكلمةِ بهِ كما يقومُ العَرَضُ بالجوهر : وهذا يُوجِبُ مفارقته لذات الجوهر الذي هو عندهم مجموع الأقانيم الثلاثة ، وهم يقولون : اتَّحد بناسوت عيسى مِنْ غيرِ أنْ يفارق ذاتَ الجوهر ، ومن المعلوم ضرورةً : أنَّ المعنى الواحد لا يقوم بذاتين .
ومنهم مَنْ فسَّرَ هذا الاتحاد بالاختلاطِ والمزج : كاختلاط الخمرِ والماء ونحوهما مِنَ المائعاتِ ، وكيفَ يُعقَلُ الاختلاط الذي هو مِنْ صفاتِ الأجسام في الكلمة التي هي معنى مِنَ المعاني ، بل هي عندهم ، وخاصية للذات الأزلية ؟!
حال
ومنهم مَنْ فَسَّرَهُ بالانطباع : كانطباع صورة النقشِ في الشمع ومعلوم أن النقش لم يحصل فيما طُبعَ فيه ، وإنَّما حصل فيه مثاله (۱) .
ولنقتصر على هذا القدْرِ مِنْ بيانِ فضائحهم ؛ فإِنَّ التطويل غيرُ لائق بالغرضِ مِنَ هذا المختصرِ ، والقومُ قد انكشف عوارُهم ، ومباديه تدلُّ على مناهيه ، ومذهبهم غير معقول ، وهم أخسُّ الفِرَقِ كلها وأرذلها أفهاماً ، وإدراك الحقائق على مثلهم عسير
Then they also claimed: that the hypostasis of knowledge from them — and it is called the Word — united with the human nature of Jesus; that is, his body, and therefore he was a god according to them. And they differed over the meaning of the union of the Word with him: Among them are those who interpreted the subsistence of the Word in him as an accident subsists in a substance: and this necessitates its separation from the essence of the substance which according to them is the collection of the three hypostases, while they say: it united with the human nature of Jesus without separating from the essence of the substance, and it is necessarily known that a single meaning does not subsist in two essences. Among them are those who interpreted this union as mixing and blending: like the mixing of wine and water and similar liquids, and how can mixing which is a property of bodies be conceived in the Word which is a meaning among meanings, rather it is according to them a property of the eternal essence?! Among them are those who interpreted it as impression: like the impression of an image on wax, and it is known that the image did not occur in that on which it was impressed, but rather its likeness occurred in it. And let us restrict ourselves to this amount of exposing their scandals; for prolixity is not fitting for the purpose of this abridgment, and the people have been exposed in their defects, and their principles indicate their ends, and their doctrine is unintelligible, and they are the most base of all sects and the meanest in understanding, and grasping realities is difficult for the likes of them.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of Christian interpretations of the hypostatic union. The author begins by stating the Christian claim: the hypostasis of knowledge (the Word) united with Jesus' human nature, making him divine. Then he examines three interpretations of this union, showing each to be absurd.

1. The first interpretation is that the Word subsists in Jesus as an accident subsists in a substance. The author points out that this would require the Word to leave the divine essence, since a single meaning cannot subsist in two distinct essences. The Christians, however, claim the union occurs without separation, which is impossible.

2. The second interpretation is mixing and blending, like liquids. The author argues that mixing is a property of bodies, but the Word is a meaning (a non-physical attribute), so it cannot be mixed.

3. The third interpretation is impression, like an image on wax. The author notes that an image does not transfer itself onto the wax; rather, a likeness appears. This does not establish a true union.

The author then concludes that these absurdities expose the Christians' errors, and their doctrine is unintelligible.

Key Terms
اتحاد
أقنوم
ناسوت
جوهر
عرض
انطباع
فضائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following interpretations of the union does Sanusi refute by stating that a single meaning cannot subsist in two essences?
A) Mixing and blending
B) Impression
C) Subsistence of an accident in a substance
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Sanusi shows that each Christian interpretation of the union leads to absurdity, thus invalidating their claim of Jesus' divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading Sanusi's logical dismantling of the three interpretations, do you find any residue of anthropomorphism in your own understanding of Allah's attributes? How can you guard against drawing flawed analogies between the Creator and creation?
?
Sanusi calls the Christian doctrine 'unintelligible'. Have you ever felt drawn to a belief or practice that seemed appealing but upon rational examination was unsound? How does this lesson strengthen your commitment to using reason in faith?
?
The passage emphasizes that 'a single meaning does not subsist in two essences'. This principle protects Tawhid. Where in your own life might you be trying to unite incompatible things—like worldly desires and spiritual devotion—that cannot coexist? What action can you take today to resolve that contradiction?

Refuting the Evidence-Based Claim for Jesus' Divinity
Sanusi refutes the Christian claim by showing flawed logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses logical consistency to refute the Christian claim that Jesus is divine based on evidence.

Core Idea
Evidence for divinity cannot be limited to Jesus without contradiction.

Sanusi engages in a dialectical argument with a Christian opponent who claims Jesus is divine because of the evidence (miracles). The opponent concedes two principles: evidence implies divinity, and absence of evidence does not prove absence. Sanusi then shows that these principles lead to absurd consequences, thereby refuting the claim. Let's trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage slowly
Read the original Arabic passage once to get a sense of the flow. Then read the translation to ensure comprehension.
2
Identify the two principles the opponent concedes
The opponent agrees that (1) existence of evidence implies existence of the evidenced, and (2) absence of evidence does not imply absence of the evidenced. Note these carefully.
These are the building blocks of the argument.
3
Follow the first application: Moses
Sanusi points out that the same evidence (miracles) existed in Moses, so by principle (1) Moses would also be divine. This challenges the uniqueness of Jesus.
4
Follow the second application: insects
When Sanusi asks if insects could be gods, the opponent denies because there is no evidence. But principle (2) says absence of evidence does not disprove divinity, so the denial is inconsistent.
This exposes the flaw in the opponent's reasoning.
5
Reflect on the outcome
The opponent is confounded. The argument does not require proving anything about Jesus or Moses directly; it only shows the opponent's own principles are inconsistent.
This method is typical of kalam dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له : قد سلَّمْتَ أَنَّهُ يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ الدليل وجود المدلول ، ودليل الألوهية على زعمكم موجود في موسى عليه السلام على حد وجودِهِ في عيسى عليه السلام ، فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ إلهاً مثلَهُ ؛ لاستحالة وجودِ الدليل دون مدلوله . ثم قلتُ له : وهل يجوز أن نكون نحنُ وهذه الحيوانات المحتقرة كالخنافس ونحوها آلهة ؟ فقالَ : لا أُجوِّرُ ذلك ؛ لعدم دليل الألوهية فقلتُ : كيف وقد سلَّمْتَ أنَّه لا يلزمُ مِنْ عدم الدليل عدمُ المدلول ، فلعلها تكونُ آلهة في نفس الأمر على مقتضى أصلكم ، ولو لم يظهر بعد دليلُ ألوهِيَّتِها ؟! ﴿ فَبُهِتَ الَّذِي كَفَرَ وَاللَّهُ لَا يَهْدِي الْقَوْمَ الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٥٨]
He said: You have conceded that from the existence of the evidence the existence of the evidenced is necessary, and the evidence of divinity according to your claim exists in Moses, peace be upon him, to the same extent as it exists in Jesus, peace be upon him, so it would be necessary that he be a god like him, because it is impossible for evidence to exist without its evidenced. Then I said to him: Is it permissible that we and these contemptible animals like beetles and the like be gods? He said: I do not allow that, due to the absence of evidence of divinity. So I said: How [can you say that] when you have conceded that from the absence of evidence the absence of the evidenced is not necessary, so perhaps they are gods in themselves according to the principle of your school, even if the evidence of their divinity has not yet appeared?! "So the one who disbelieved was confounded, and Allah does not guide the wrongdoing people." (al-Baqarah 2:258)
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents a logical argument against the Christian claim that Jesus is divine based on evidences or miracles. The opponent concedes that if evidence of divinity exists in someone, then that person must be divine. The speaker then points out that the same evidence (miracles) existed in Moses, so by that logic Moses would also be divine. The opponent is then asked whether common creatures could be gods if we accept that absence of evidence does not imply absence of divinity. The opponent rejects that because there is no evidence, but the speaker reminds him that his own principle says lack of evidence does not prove non-existence, so the creatures might be gods. The argument shows the inconsistency of the opponent's position, and the verse concludes that the disbeliever was confounded.

Key Terms
دليل
مدلول
ألوهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles alone prove divinity without needing logical consistency.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if miracles are evidence, then all miracle workers would be divine, which is absurd; and absence of evidence does not disprove divinity, so one cannot reject others on that basis.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does Sanusi expose in the opponent's reasoning about evidence of divinity?
A) The opponent cannot prove that Moses had no evidence.
B) The opponent's principle that evidence implies divinity would also apply to Moses, and his refusal to accept insects as gods contradicts his own claim that absence of evidence does not prove absence of divinity.
C) The opponent admits that evidence in Jesus is unique.
D) The opponent refuses to answer the question about insects.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the claim of Jesus' divinity by showing that the opponent's own logical principles lead to absurd or contradictory conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there principles you hold in one area of your life that you inconsistently apply in another? Examine one such area today and commit to aligning your actions with your stated beliefs.
?
When you are faced with contradictory evidence regarding a deeply held belief, do you tend to rationalize inconsistencies rather than reconsider the belief? This lesson challenges you to value truth over comfort—will you accept correction if your reasoning is flawed?
?
The opponent in this passage was silenced but not necessarily convinced. Are there beliefs you cling to despite clear logical flaws? Ask yourself: would you rather be confounded and humbled, or persist in error? What step will you take today to ensure your faith is built on sound reasoning?

The Christian Claim on Incarnation and Atonement
Sanusi reports the Christian claim for why the Word united with Jesus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Christian rationale for the incarnation and atonement as presented by Sanusi, and why he considers it absurd.

Core Idea
The Christian atonement story is built on a misunderstanding of divine transcendence.

Sanusi reports a Christian argument that tries to explain why the Word (divine nature) united with Jesus. He frames it with a sharp comment about their stupidity. This lesson presents that claim before his refutation. As you read, notice the reasoning they give and the assumptions they make about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify key elements of the Christian claim
Identify the four key elements: Adam's sin and deserved punishment, the problem of punishing a non-peer, the incarnation of the Word making Jesus divine, and Jesus offering himself as substitute.
These are the building blocks of their argument.
3
Reflect on Sanusi's framing
Sanusi begins by saying 'great stupidity'. Ask yourself why he says that. What assumptions about God are being made by the Christians?
This connects to the broader Islamic critique of the incarnation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : وانظر عظيمَ غباوتهم فيما تخيَّلوهُ مِنْ حكمة كونِ عيسى عليهِ السلامُ اتَّحدَ بهِ اللاهوتُ حتى كانَ إلها عندهم ، ثم طُلِبَ بعد ذلك على زعمهم ، قالوا - أبعدَهُمُ اللهُ تعالى وأخلى منهم الأرضَ - : وحكمة ذلكَ : أَنَّ آدمَ أبا البشرِ عليهِ السلامُ لمَّا أَكلَ مِنَ الشجرةِ وعصى أمرَ رَبِّهِ .. استحق العقوبةَ مِنْ ربَّهِ ، لكن عقوبة المولى على ما هو عليهِ مِنْ عظيم الجلالِ لَمَنْ ليسَ نظيراً له .. فيه نقص بهِ ، قالوا : فلما اتَّحدَتِ الكلمةُ بعيسى عليه السلام ، ورجع بسببها إلها . . تكرَّمَ بنفسِهِ وبذلها للعقوبة نيابةً عن أبيهِ آدَمَ عليهِ السلامُ ، ولم يكن في إيقاعها بهِ نقص في الإله لمشاكلتِهِ لهُ ؛ إذْ هو إله مثلُهُ قالوا : فهذا هو حكمة قتله وصلبه !
I said: And consider the great stupidity in what they imagined as the wisdom of the fact that Jesus, peace be upon him, was united with the divine nature (al-lāhūt) so that he became a god according to them, then he was sought thereafter according to their claim. They said — may Allah Almighty distance them and empty the earth of them —: And the wisdom of that is: When Adam, the father of mankind, peace be upon him, ate from the tree and disobeyed the command of his Lord, he deserved punishment from his Lord, but the punishment of the Master, given what He is of great majesty, upon one who is not His peer, involves a deficiency in Him. They said: So when the Word (al-kalima) united with Jesus, peace be upon him, and he became a god thereby, he generously offered himself and gave himself up for punishment on behalf of his father Adam, peace be upon him, and there was no deficiency in the god in inflicting it upon him because of his resemblance to Him; since he is a god like Him. They said: This is the wisdom of his killing and crucifixion!
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage reports the Christian theological claim for the incarnation and atonement. The Christians assert that after Adam's sin, punishment was due, but for God to punish a non-peer (a mere human) would be a deficiency in God. Therefore, the Word (divine nature) united with Jesus, making him a god, and then he voluntarily offered himself as a substitute punishment for Adam. Since Jesus is divine and thus a peer of God, no deficiency occurs. This is presented as the rationale for the crucifixion. Sanusi begins by noting the 'great stupidity' of this idea, setting up the refutation that follows.

Key Terms
اللاهوت
الناسوت
الكلمة
اتَّحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers might think Sanusi is endorsing this Christian claim because he reports it without immediate refutation.
Correction: Sanusi begins by saying 'great stupidity', indicating he is reporting it to refute it. The passage is a presentation of the opposing view before the refutation.

Checkpoint
According to the Christian claim reported here, why did the Word unite with Jesus?
A) To show God's power over nature.
B) So that Jesus could be punished as a substitute for Adam, and since Jesus is divine, the punishment would not be a deficiency in God.
C) To make Jesus a prophet.
D) To fulfill a prophecy.

Key Takeaway
The Christian claim for atonement hinges on making Jesus divine to avoid deficiency in God, but Sanusi considers this absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever subconsciously thought that your sins are too great for God to forgive without some 'payment' from you? How does the arrogance of the Christian atonement story — inventing a divine sacrifice to 'fix' God's justice — expose the hidden pride in your own heart when you doubt His mercy? What will you do today to repent with full trust in His forgiveness, not in your own efforts?
?
Sanusi calls their reasoning 'great stupidity.' But are there any beliefs you hold about God that actually limit His majesty — like thinking He needs your worship, or that He is bound to reward your good deeds? This lesson forces you to ask: Do I truly believe Allah is free and independent, or do I secretly try to put Him in a box? Will you spend five minutes today listing ways you might be 'boxing' God and then actively reject those thoughts?
?
The Christians argued that punishing a non-peer would be 'deficient' for God. Do you ever act as if God owes you something — like a good life because you are Muslim, or forgiveness because you prayed? How does this passage's stark exposure of human arrogance over the nature of God challenge you to examine your own expectations of Him? Commit today to recite 'SubhanAllah' with the meaning that He is utterly free of all deficiency and need.

First dilemma: only the human nature suffered
Sanusi presents the first horn of the dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first dilemma: if only the human nature of Jesus suffered the crucifixion, then it contradicts Christian claims about divine justice and the fusion of natures.

Core Idea
If only the human nature suffered, God's justice and the fusion of natures are undermined.

Sanusi begins his refutation by posing a crucial question: did the crucifixion affect only Jesus' human nature or both? He first examines the possibility that only the human nature suffered, showing how it leads to contradictions within Christian theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the first dilemma
Read the Arabic passage for the first horn of the dilemma. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi presents the conditional and its consequences.
Focus on the logical flow: if only human nature suffered, then...
2
Identify the two objections
Sanusi gives two reasons why this possibility fails. First, it contradicts the Christian claim that punishing a non-peer is a deficiency. Second, it is incompatible with the fusion of natures.
Think about why each objection is considered fatal.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this dilemma undermines the Christian narrative of the crucifixion as a sacrifice for sin. Why is the human nature alone insufficient?
This connects to the broader Islamic critique of the crucifixion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقيل لهم : هذا القتل والصلب الذي زعمتُم وقوعَهُ بهِ : هل انفردَ به الناسوت دونَ اللاهوتِ ، أو نالهما معاً ؟ فإن قلتُمُ : انفردَ بهِ ناسوت عيسى فقط .. انتقض عليكم ما قلتُموهُ : إِنَّ إيقاع الإلهِ العقوبة بمَنْ ليس نظيراً له نقص بهِ ، ولا شكّ أنَّ الناسوت وهو جسد عيسى عليه السلام ليس بإله قطعاً وأيضاً : فكيف ينفرد الناسوتُ بذلك القتل والصلب معَ القولِ بامتزاجه مع اللاهوت ؟! وإن قلتُم : إِنَّ القتل والصلب نال المجموع مِنَ اللاهوت والناسوت .. لزمَ أنَّ الإله يلحقه الموتُ والألم وغيرُ ذلكَ ممَّا يلحق المخلوق ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثه ضرورةً ، وهو محال قطعاً وأيضاً : فذلك يؤدّي إلى انعدام الإله الذي هو مُركَّبٌ عندَكُم مِنَ الأقانيم الثلاثةِ ؛ إذ المركَّبُ ينعدم بانعدام جزئهِ ، وقد انعدم جزء الإله الذي حلَّ بعيسى لقتلِهِ معَهُ ، فقدِ انعدم إذا الإله ، فلم يبقَ بعد ذلك إله !
So it was said to them: This killing and crucifixion which you claim occurred to him — did the human nature (an-nāsūt) alone suffer it without the divine nature (al-lāhūt), or did it befall both together? If you say: The human nature of Jesus alone suffered it — then what you said is invalidated: that the infliction of punishment by God upon one who is not His peer is a deficiency in Him; and there is no doubt that the human nature, which is the body of Jesus, peace be upon him, is definitely not a god. Also, how can the human nature alone suffer that killing and crucifixion while asserting its fusion with the divine nature?! And if you say: The killing and crucifixion befell the combination of the divine and human natures — then it would follow that death, pain, and other things that affect creatures affect God, and that necessarily entails His temporal origination (ḥudūth), which is absolutely impossible. Also, that leads to the annihilation of the god who, according to you, is composed of three hypostases (aqānīm); for a composite ceases to exist with the cessation of its part, and the part of the god which indwelt Jesus has ceased to exist due to his being killed with it, so the god has ceased to exist, and no god remains after that.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation of the Christian claim through two conditional dilemmas. The refutation begins by asking a crucial question: Did the crucifixion affect only Jesus' human nature (nāsūt) or both the human and divine natures (lāhūt) together? 

First horn: If only the human nature suffered, then the Christian justification collapses because they claimed punishment of a non-peer would be a deficiency; the human nature is not God, so punishing it is indeed a deficiency. Moreover, if the natures are fused, it is impossible for one to suffer without the other. 

Second horn: If both natures suffered, then God would experience death and pain, which are properties of contingent creatures, implying that God is created (ḥādith), which is impossible. Furthermore, the Trinitarian god is composite (three hypostases), and if one part (the Word that indwelt Jesus) is destroyed by death, then the composite god is annihilated, leaving no god. Thus, the Christian doctrine leads to absurdities and contradictions.

Key Terms
الناسوت
اللاهوت
القتل
الصلب
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the historical event of the crucifixion.
Correction: Sanusi's argument is a logical critique of Christian theological claims, not a denial or affirmation of the historical event.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence Sanusi argues if only the human nature of Jesus suffered the crucifixion?
A) The human nature is divine, so punishment is not a deficiency.
B) It would be a deficiency for God to punish a non-peer, and the fusion of natures would be violated.
C) The divine nature would have to die, which is impossible.
D) The crucifixion would then be ineffective.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's first dilemma shows that if only the human nature suffered, Christian claims about divine justice and the fusion of natures lead to contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows a belief (the crucifixion) that, when examined logically, collapses into contradiction. Can you name one belief you personally hold that you have never honestly examined for logical consistency? What would it take to scrutinize it as Sanusi scrutinizes the Christian claim? Will you commit to examining it this week?
?
Sanusi uses the Christians' own principle against them. Is there an area in your life where your actions or beliefs contradict a standard you claim to uphold? For example, do you claim to trust in God's justice but then despair when things go wrong? How does this lesson call you to align your inner state with your professed beliefs? Write one specific step you will take.
?
The argument focuses on the grave consequence of attributing deficiency to God. Pause and ask yourself: In your daily thoughts, do you ever inadvertently attribute imperfection to God—by complaining, by doubting His wisdom, or by acting as if He is not in control? Confess this to yourself and commit to replacing one such thought today with a statement of His perfection.

Second dilemma: both natures suffered
Sanusi presents the second horn of the dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's second dilemma: if both natures suffered, then God would experience death and the Trinity would be destroyed.

Core Idea
If both natures suffered, God becomes temporal and the composite Trinity is annihilated.

Sanusi now examines the other possibility: that both the human and divine natures suffered the crucifixion. He shows that this leads to even more severe consequences, including the implication that God is created and the Trinity ceases to exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the second dilemma
Read the Arabic passage for the second horn. Notice how Sanusi presents the conditional and then draws two conclusions.
Focus on the transition from the first horn.
2
Understand the first consequence
If both natures suffered, then God (the divine nature) would experience death and pain — attributes of creatures. This implies God is created (ḥādith), which is impossible for the divine.
This is a direct attack on divine impassibility.
3
Understand the second consequence
The second consequence is that the composite Trinity would be annihilated. Since the Word (a hypostasis) indwelt Jesus and was killed, the composite god ceases to exist.
This exploits the Trinitarian concept of composition.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Sanusi uses logical reasoning to show that both options lead to absurdities, thereby refuting the Christian claim of the crucifixion.
This is a classic example of reductio ad absurdum.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقيل لهم : هذا القتل والصلب الذي زعمتُم وقوعَهُ بهِ : هل انفردَ به الناسوت دونَ اللاهوتِ ، أو نالهما معاً ؟ فإن قلتُمُ : انفردَ بهِ ناسوت عيسى فقط .. انتقض عليكم ما قلتُموهُ : إِنَّ إيقاع الإلهِ العقوبة بمَنْ ليس نظيراً له نقص بهِ ، ولا شكّ أنَّ الناسوت وهو جسد عيسى عليه السلام ليس بإله قطعاً وأيضاً : فكيف ينفرد الناسوتُ بذلك القتل والصلب معَ القولِ بامتزاجه مع اللاهوت ؟! وإن قلتُم : إِنَّ القتل والصلب نال المجموع مِنَ اللاهوت والناسوت .. لزمَ أنَّ الإله يلحقه الموتُ والألم وغيرُ ذلكَ ممَّا يلحق المخلوق ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثه ضرورةً ، وهو محال قطعاً وأيضاً : فذلك يؤدّي إلى انعدام الإله الذي هو مُركَّبٌ عندَكُم مِنَ الأقانيم الثلاثةِ ؛ إذ المركَّبُ ينعدم بانعدام جزئهِ ، وقد انعدم جزء الإله الذي حلَّ بعيسى لقتلِهِ معَهُ ، فقدِ انعدم إذا الإله ، فلم يبقَ بعد ذلك إله !
So it was said to them: This killing and crucifixion which you claim occurred to him — did the human nature (an-nāsūt) alone suffer it without the divine nature (al-lāhūt), or did it befall both together? If you say: The human nature of Jesus alone suffered it — then what you said is invalidated: that the infliction of punishment by God upon one who is not His peer is a deficiency in Him; and there is no doubt that the human nature, which is the body of Jesus, peace be upon him, is definitely not a god. Also, how can the human nature alone suffer that killing and crucifixion while asserting its fusion with the divine nature?! And if you say: The killing and crucifixion befell the combination of the divine and human natures — then it would follow that death, pain, and other things that affect creatures affect God, and that necessarily entails His temporal origination (ḥudūth), which is absolutely impossible. Also, that leads to the annihilation of the god who, according to you, is composed of three hypostases (aqānīm); for a composite ceases to exist with the cessation of its part, and the part of the god which indwelt Jesus has ceased to exist due to his being killed with it, so the god has ceased to exist, and no god remains after that.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation of the Christian claim through two conditional dilemmas. The refutation begins by asking a crucial question: Did the crucifixion affect only Jesus' human nature (nāsūt) or both the human and divine natures (lāhūt) together? 

First horn: If only the human nature suffered, then the Christian justification collapses because they claimed punishment of a non-peer would be a deficiency; the human nature is not God, so punishing it is indeed a deficiency. Moreover, if the natures are fused, it is impossible for one to suffer without the other. 

Second horn: If both natures suffered, then God would experience death and pain, which are properties of contingent creatures, implying that God is created (ḥādith), which is impossible. Furthermore, the Trinitarian god is composite (three hypostases), and if one part (the Word that indwelt Jesus) is destroyed by death, then the composite god is annihilated, leaving no god. Thus, the Christian doctrine leads to absurdities and contradictions.

Key Terms
اللاهوت
القتل
الصلب
حدوث
الأقانيم
المركَّب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the historical fact of the crucifixion.
Correction: Sanusi is critiquing the logical coherence of Christian theology, not debating history.

Checkpoint
What is one consequence Sanusi argues if both natures of Jesus suffered the crucifixion?
A) God would be immortal and unaffected.
B) God would become temporal and the composite Trinity would be annihilated.
C) Only the human nature would die, preserving God.
D) The crucifixion would be a temporary event.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's second dilemma shows that if both natures suffered, God would be subject to creation and the Trinity would self-destruct, making the Christian position impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot suffer or change in any way, how does that confront the way you sometimes imagine His anger or mercy as fluctuating with your actions? This lesson demands that you recalibrate your emotional conception of God to one of absolute perfection.
?
Sanusi's argument shows that a composite god can be destroyed. Do you ever treat the oneness of God as a mere belief rather than a lived reality that permeates your trust and reliance? How does this passage push you to deepen your tawḥīd beyond lip service?
?
The idea that God would experience death is absurd. But do you ever, in difficult moments, mentally attribute helplessness to God? This lesson insists that God is beyond all change and need. Will you consciously repent of any thought that diminishes His transcendence?

The First Horn: Only Human Nature Suffered
Sanusi presents the Christian claim and then introduces the dilemma, focusing first on the possibility that only the human nature suffered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's refutation of the claim that only Christ's human nature suffered in the crucifixion, showing it contradicts the Christian premise of divine justice and the hypostatic union.

Core Idea
If only human nature suffered, the victim was not God—undermining the claim of just punishment.

Sanusi begins by presenting the Christian justification for the crucifixion and then poses a critical question: did the suffering affect only the human nature or both? In this lesson, we examine the first possibility and Sanusi's two arguments against it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Christian claim and the dilemma
Read the first part where Sanusi states the Christian argument and then introduces the dilemma. Notice the two options.
2
Identify the first option and Sanusi's first objection
Find where Sanusi says 'If you say: Only the human nature...' and then his first objection about punishing a non-peer.
3
Identify the second objection based on union
Read the second objection that asks how the human nature alone can suffer if it is united with the divine nature.
This uses the Christian premise of hypostatic union against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبذلها للعقوبة نيابةً عن أبيهِ آدَمَ عليهِ السلامُ ، ولم يكن في إيقاعها بهِ نقص في الإله لمشاكلتِهِ لهُ ؛ إذْ هو إله مثلُهُ قالوا : فهذا هو حكمة قتله وصلبه ! فقيل لهم : هذا القتل والصلب الذي زعمتُم وقوعَهُ بهِ : هل انفردَ به الناسوت دونَ اللاهوتِ ، أو نالهما معاً ؟ فإن قلتُمُ : انفردَ بهِ ناسوت عيسى فقط .. انتقض عليكم ما قلتُموهُ : إِنَّ إيقاع الإلهِ العقوبة بمَنْ ليس نظيراً له نقص بهِ ، ولا شكّ أنَّ الناسوت وهو جسد عيسى عليه السلام ليس بإله قطعاً وأيضاً : فكيف ينفرد الناسوتُ بذلك القتل والصلب معَ القولِ بامتزاجه مع اللاهوت ؟! وإن قلتُم : إِنَّ القتل والصلب نال المجموع مِنَ اللاهوت والناسوت .. لزمَ أنَّ الإله يلحقه الموتُ والألم وغيرُ ذلكَ ممَّا يلحق المخلوق ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثه ضرورةً ، وهو محال قطعاً وأيضاً : فذلك يؤدّي إلى انعدام الإله الذي هو مُركَّبٌ عندَكُم مِنَ الأقانيم الثلاثةِ ؛ إذ المركَّبُ ينعدم بانعدام جزئهِ ، وقد انعدم جزء الإله الذي حلَّ بعيسى لقتلِهِ معَهُ ، فقدِ انعدم إذا الإله ، فلم يبقَ بعد ذلك إله ! فتباً لعقول هؤلاء الحمير فما أخشها ! عقول صغيرة خسيسة تحملها أجسام كبيرةٌ ، ﴿ وَإِذَا رَأَيْنَهُمْ تُعْجِبُكَ أَجْسَامُهُمْ وَإِن يَقُولُوا تَسْمَعْ لِقَوْلِهِمْ كَأَنَّهُمْ خُشُبُ مُّسَنَدَهُ ﴾ [المنافقون : ٤] ، نفوس بهيميَّةٌ حملتها هياكل إنسانية ، ﴿ إِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا كَالأَنْم بَلْ هُمْ أَضَلُّ سَبِيلًا) [الفرقان : ٤٤] . اللهم ؛ إِنَّا نستودعُكَ ديننا ودينَ أحبَّتِنا ، ونعوذُ بكَ مِنْ سلبِ المعرفة ، ومسخ القلوب فاحفظنا بما حفظت بهِ أصفياءَكَ ، حتى نلقاك وأنت عنا راض ، يا مَنْ لا تخيب ودائعُهُ ، ولا يضيعُ مَنِ اتَّكل عليه ، يا ذا الجلال والإكرام . وأيضاً : فآل الأمرُ على هذا الفرْضِ - وهو أنَّ العقوبة بالقتل والصلب نالَتِ اللاهوت والناسوت - إلى أنَّ الإله انتقم بنفسِهِ مِنْ نفسِهِ ، وعاقبها في معصية صدرَتْ مِنْ عبدِهِ ! فانظر هذا الهوس وهذا التلاعب والهذيان الذي ابتلي به هؤلاء القوم ، ولا حول ولا قوَّةَ إلا بالله ، وقُلِ : الحمدُ للهِ الذي عافانا ممَّا ابتلاهم بهِ ، وفضَّلَنا على كثيرٍ ممَّنْ خلق تفضيلاً . اللهم ؛ كما بدأت بالإنعام بمحْض فضلِكَ فأتمم لنا ذلك يا مولانا بحسْنِ الخاتمة ، والصفح عن جميع الذنوب بلا محنة دنيا وأخرى ، يا أرحم الراحمين .
And they offered it as punishment on behalf of his father Adam, peace be upon him, and there was no deficiency in the God in inflicting it on him because of his similarity to Him, since he is a god like Him. They said: This is the wisdom of killing him and crucifying him! So it was said to them: This killing and crucifixion that you claim occurred to him: did only the human nature (al-nāsūt) alone receive it without the divine nature (al-lāhūt), or did both receive it together? If you say: Only the human nature of Jesus received it... then what you said is contradicted: 'Indeed, the God inflicting punishment on one who is not His peer is a deficiency in Him,' and there is no doubt that the human nature, which is the body of Jesus, peace be upon him, is definitely not a god. Also: How can the human nature alone receive that killing and crucifixion with the claim of its mixture with the divine nature?! And if you say: The killing and crucifixion affected the composite of the divine and human natures... then it follows that death, pain, and other things that affect the created being affect the God, and that necessarily entails His temporality (ḥudūth), which is absolutely impossible. Also: That leads to the annihilation of the God who, according to you, is composed of three hypostases (al-aqānīm al-thalātha); for the composite is annihilated by the annihilation of its part, and the part of the God that dwelt in Jesus has been annihilated due to his being killed with it, so the God has thus been annihilated, and after that there remains no god! So shame on the minds of these donkeys—how base they are! Small, vile minds carried by large bodies, {And when you see them, their bodies please you, and if they speak, you listen to their speech; they are like propped-up planks of wood} [Al-Munāfiqūn: 4], bestial souls carried by human frames, {They are but like cattle; rather, they are more astray in way} [Al-Furqān: 44]. O Allah, indeed we entrust to You our religion and the religion of our loved ones, and we seek refuge in You from the removal of knowledge and the corruption of hearts, so preserve us as You have preserved Your chosen ones, until we meet You while You are pleased with us, O He whose deposits are never lost, and he who relies on Him is not wasted, O Possessor of Majesty and Honor. Also: The matter comes down to this assumption—that the punishment of killing and crucifixion affected the divine and human natures—to the point that the God took revenge on Himself from Himself, and punished Himself for a sin committed by His servant! So look at this delirium, this manipulation, and this raving that these people are afflicted with, and there is no power or strength except with Allah, and say: Praise be to Allah who has spared us from what He has afflicted them with, and has favored us over many of His creatures greatly. O Allah, just as You began with favor by Your pure grace, complete that for us, O our Master, with a good end, and pardon for all sins without trial in this world and the next, O Most Merciful of the merciful.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Christian claim that the crucifixion of Jesus was just because God punished sin in a being equal to Himself (Jesus being God). The author presents a dilemma: did the crucifixion affect only the human nature (nāsūt) of Jesus, or both the human and divine natures (lāhūt)? If only the human nature, then the Christian argument fails because the human nature is not God, so punishing a non-equal would be a deficiency. Moreover, if the two natures are united (hypostatic union), how could the human nature alone suffer? If both natures suffered, then God would be subject to death and pain, implying createdness, which is impossible. Additionally, the Trinity as a composite would be annihilated if one part (the divine nature in Jesus) ceased to exist. The author then launches a rhetorical condemnation, quoting Quranic verses to disparage their intellect. Finally, a further consequence is drawn: if both natures suffered, then God punished Himself for the sin of His servant, which is absurd. The passage ends with supplications and praise to Allah for guidance.

Key Terms
الناسوت
اللاهوت
القتل والصلب
العقوبة
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, if only the human nature of Jesus suffered in the crucifixion, what is the first problem?
A) It would show that God is not just.
B) It would entail that the human nature is not God, so punishing a non-peer is a deficiency.
C) It would prove the union of natures.
D) It would confirm the wisdom of the crucifixion.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi effectively refutes the possibility that only Christ's human nature was crucified by showing it undermines the Christian claim of divine justice and the hypostatic union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that the Christian claim of just punishment for sin depends on punishing a peer of God. If you believe God is just, but also believe He can punish someone who is not your equal (like a sinner), how do you reconcile that? What does this passage demand you reconsider about your own understanding of divine justice?
?
Sanusi uses his opponent's own premises to expose an inconsistency. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold that, when examined carefully, contradict each other? Will you have the courage to follow the logic where it leads, even if it means giving up a comfortable idea?
?
The crucifixion narrative assumes that suffering can be transferred from the guilty to the innocent. What does this imply about responsibility and justice? How does this passage challenge you to think about personal accountability before God, especially regarding sins you might hope others can bear for you?

The Second Horn: Both Natures Suffered
Sanusi explores the consequences if both natures suffered, listing three absurdities and then condemning the doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's arguments against the possibility that both natures of Christ suffered in the crucifixion, leading to conclusions that contradict divine attributes and the Trinity.

Core Idea
If both natures suffered, God would be temporal, the Trinity annihilated, and God punishing Himself—all absurd.

In this lesson, we examine the second horn of Sanusi's dilemma. Assuming the crucifixion affected both the divine and human natures, Sanusi draws three devastating consequences: God would be subject to death and temporality, the composite Trinity would be destroyed if one part perishes, and God would be punishing Himself for the sin of His servant. Each of these is impossible for the divine nature. The passage concludes with a rhetorical condemnation and a supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the second horn and first consequence
Read the part starting 'If you say: The killing and crucifixion affected the composite...' and note the first consequence: God would be subject to death, implying temporality.
2
Identify the second consequence
Find where Sanusi says 'Also: That leads to the annihilation of the God...' and understand how the composite Trinity would be annihilated.
3
Read the third consequence and conclusion
Read the final argument about God punishing Himself and then the rhetorical condemnation and supplication.
Reflect on how Sanusi uses the Christian premises internally.

وبذلها للعقوبة نيابةً عن أبيهِ آدَمَ عليهِ السلامُ ، ولم يكن في إيقاعها بهِ نقص في الإله لمشاكلتِهِ لهُ ؛ إذْ هو إله مثلُهُ قالوا : فهذا هو حكمة قتله وصلبه ! فقيل لهم : هذا القتل والصلب الذي زعمتُم وقوعَهُ بهِ : هل انفردَ به الناسوت دونَ اللاهوتِ ، أو نالهما معاً ؟ فإن قلتُمُ : انفردَ بهِ ناسوت عيسى فقط .. انتقض عليكم ما قلتُموهُ : إِنَّ إيقاع الإلهِ العقوبة بمَنْ ليس نظيراً له نقص بهِ ، ولا شكّ أنَّ الناسوت وهو جسد عيسى عليه السلام ليس بإله قطعاً وأيضاً : فكيف ينفرد الناسوتُ بذلك القتل والصلب معَ القولِ بامتزاجه مع اللاهوت ؟! وإن قلتُم : إِنَّ القتل والصلب نال المجموع مِنَ اللاهوت والناسوت .. لزمَ أنَّ الإله يلحقه الموتُ والألم وغيرُ ذلكَ ممَّا يلحق المخلوق ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثه ضرورةً ، وهو محال قطعاً وأيضاً : فذلك يؤدّي إلى انعدام الإله الذي هو مُركَّبٌ عندَكُم مِنَ الأقانيم الثلاثةِ ؛ إذ المركَّبُ ينعدم بانعدام جزئهِ ، وقد انعدم جزء الإله الذي حلَّ بعيسى لقتلِهِ معَهُ ، فقدِ انعدم إذا الإله ، فلم يبقَ بعد ذلك إله ! فتباً لعقول هؤلاء الحمير فما أخشها ! عقول صغيرة خسيسة تحملها أجسام كبيرةٌ ، ﴿ وَإِذَا رَأَيْنَهُمْ تُعْجِبُكَ أَجْسَامُهُمْ وَإِن يَقُولُوا تَسْمَعْ لِقَوْلِهِمْ كَأَنَّهُمْ خُشُبُ مُّسَنَدَهُ ﴾ [المنافقون : ٤] ، نفوس بهيميَّةٌ حملتها هياكل إنسانية ، ﴿ إِنْ هُمْ إِلَّا كَالأَنْم بَلْ هُمْ أَضَلُّ سَبِيلًا) [الفرقان : ٤٤] . اللهم ؛ إِنَّا نستودعُكَ ديننا ودينَ أحبَّتِنا ، ونعوذُ بكَ مِنْ سلبِ المعرفة ، ومسخ القلوب فاحفظنا بما حفظت بهِ أصفياءَكَ ، حتى نلقاك وأنت عنا راض ، يا مَنْ لا تخيب ودائعُهُ ، ولا يضيعُ مَنِ اتَّكل عليه ، يا ذا الجلال والإكرام . وأيضاً : فآل الأمرُ على هذا الفرْضِ - وهو أنَّ العقوبة بالقتل والصلب نالَتِ اللاهوت والناسوت - إلى أنَّ الإله انتقم بنفسِهِ مِنْ نفسِهِ ، وعاقبها في معصية صدرَتْ مِنْ عبدِهِ ! فانظر هذا الهوس وهذا التلاعب والهذيان الذي ابتلي به هؤلاء القوم ، ولا حول ولا قوَّةَ إلا بالله ، وقُلِ : الحمدُ للهِ الذي عافانا ممَّا ابتلاهم بهِ ، وفضَّلَنا على كثيرٍ ممَّنْ خلق تفضيلاً . اللهم ؛ كما بدأت بالإنعام بمحْض فضلِكَ فأتمم لنا ذلك يا مولانا بحسْنِ الخاتمة ، والصفح عن جميع الذنوب بلا محنة دنيا وأخرى ، يا أرحم الراحمين .

The author presents a dilemma: did the crucifixion affect only the human nature (nāsūt) of Jesus, or both the human and divine natures (lāhūt)? If only the human nature, then the Christian argument fails because the human nature is not God, so punishing a non-equal would be a deficiency. Moreover, if the two natures are united (hypostatic union), how could the human nature alone suffer? If both natures suffered, then God would be subject to death and pain, implying createdness, which is impossible. Additionally, the Trinity as a composite would be annihilated if one part (the divine nature in Jesus) ceased to exist. The author then launches a rhetorical condemnation, quoting Quranic verses to disparage their intellect. Finally, a further consequence is drawn: if both natures suffered, then God punished Himself for the sin of His servant, which is absurd. The passage ends with supplications and praise to Allah for guidance.

Key Terms
اللاهوت
الناسوت
الأقانيم الثلاثة
انعدام الإله
الحدوث
الامتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the second consequence if both natures suffered in the crucifixion?
A) God would be subject to death and pain.
B) The Trinity would be annihilated because the composite would lose a part.
C) God would be punishing Himself.
D) The crucifixion would be justified.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi concludes that the second horn leads to multiple absurdities that are impossible for God, thus the crucifixion doctrine is rationally untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an argument that seems logically sound but leads to a conclusion about Allah that diminishes His perfection, do you have the courage to reject it—even if it is popular or familiar? Examine your own soul: have you ever accepted a flawed understanding of Allah simply because it was comfortable? What will you do today to uproot any such error?
?
Sanusi calls those who hold such beliefs 'donkeys' and 'bestial souls.' But before you distance yourself from them, ask: have you ever allowed your own intellect to wander into territory that contradicts the clear teachings of revelation? In what area of your faith are you most vulnerable to rationalizing away Allah's transcendence? Commit now to a specific act of seeking knowledge to guard your heart.
?
The doctrine implies that God would punish Himself for a servant's sin. Reflect: Do you truly believe that Allah is completely free from need and absolutely just, or is there a part of you that thinks He 'owes' something to creation? How does this lesson reshape your gratitude for Allah's forgiveness, which requires no sacrifice but His mercy? What will you do today to thank Him for that mercy?

The Absurdity of God Punishing Himself
Final argument against Christian union and a prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical absurdity that results from the Christian claim that punishment struck both the divine and human natures in the crucifixion.

Core Idea
If God punished Himself for a servant's sin, the doctrine self-destructs.

This passage delivers the final blow to the Christian doctrine of union (ittihad) by exposing its inner contradiction. After quoting Quranic verses that liken such beliefs to bestiality, the author offers a prayer for preservation before presenting a devastating reductio ad absurdum. The lesson will walk through this argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic characterization
Begin by reading the two Quranic verses (63:4 and 25:44) that the author uses to describe those who hold this doctrine. Notice the strong language ('propped-up pieces of wood', 'cattle').
These verses set the tone for the refutation.
2
Observe the prayer for protection
The author then entrusts his religion to Allah and seeks refuge from removal of knowledge and metamorphosis of hearts. This is a prelude to the argument.
3
Analyze the reductio argument
The core argument: If the punishment of killing and crucifixion affected both the divine and human natures, then God would be taking vengeance from Himself and punishing Himself for a sin committed by His servant. Identify the premise and the absurd conclusion.
This is the key logical move.
4
Reflect on the concluding praise and prayer
The author thanks Allah for saving Muslims from such beliefs and prays for a good end. Consider how this conclusion reinforces the lesson's objective.
The footnote references al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar's refutation of the same issue.

(۱) وقد اعتنى القاضي عبد الجبار الهمذاني المعتزلي في الرد على مسألة الاتحاد التي زعمها النصارى في ( المغني في أبواب التوحيد والعدل » ( ٥ / ١١٩ ) ، وهو جزء الفرق غير الإسلامية ، ومسألة الحلول ( ٥ / ١٢٣ ) .
For their saying: 'As if they were propped-up pieces of wood' [Quran 63:4], bestial souls carried by human structures, 'They are only like cattle, nay, they are further astray from the way' [Quran 25:44]. O Allah, we entrust to You our religion and the religion of our loved ones, and we seek refuge in You from the removal of knowledge and the metamorphosis of hearts, so preserve us as You have preserved Your chosen ones, until we meet You while You are pleased with us, O He whose deposits never fail, and he who relies upon Him is not lost, O Possessor of Majesty and Bounty. Also: the outcome of this assumption – that the punishment of killing and crucifixion affected the divine and human natures – leads to the conclusion that the God took vengeance from Himself, and punished Himself for a disobedience committed by His servant! So consider this delirium, this tampering, and this raving that these people have been afflicted with. There is no power or strength except with Allah. Say: Praise be to Allah who has saved us from what He has afflicted them with, and preferred us over many of His creation. O Allah, as You began with favor by Your sheer grace, complete that for us, O our Master, with a good end, and pardon for all sins without tribulation in this world or the next, O Most Merciful of the merciful. (1) Al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadhani al-Mu'tazili paid attention to refuting the issue of union (al-ittihad) which the Christians claimed in 'al-Mughni fi abwab al-tawhid wa al-'adl' (5/119), which is the part on non-Islamic sects, and the issue of indwelling (al-hulul) (5/123).

This passage concludes a refutation of the Christian doctrine of union (ittihad) and the claim that the divine nature (lahut) was punished along with the human nature (nasut) in the crucifixion. Refutation of the Hashwiyya’s Claim on Divine Speech
Sanusi refutes the Hashwiyya claim that God speaks and then is silent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that Allah speaks and then is silent (as alleged by the Hashwiyya) is biblically, rationally, and theologically untenable within Sunni kalam.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal and unchanging—silence cannot touch it.

This passage tackles a deviant view from a sect called al-Ḥashwiyya, who claimed that Allah sometimes speaks and sometimes remains silent, hiding His speech rather than stopping it. Sanusi denounces this as sheer nonsense and directs the serious student to a larger commentary for more exposure of their errors. Let's examine why this claim contradicts the established doctrine of God’s eternal attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the tone and content.
Notice the strong language: 'may God distance them' and 'nonsense'.
2
Identify the Hashwiyya claim
What exactly do they assert about God's speech? They say God speaks at times and is silent at other times, but silence does not mean speech ceases—He hides it.
3
Understand the author’s refutation
Sanusi dismisses the claim as 'hawas' (nonsense) that no believer or rational person would accept, and points to a larger work for more details.
The refutation relies on the earlier proof that divine speech is eternal and immutable.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Why is it important that God's speech is not subject to silence? Because if speech could cease or be hidden, it would imply change in God, which is impossible.
This connects to the doctrine of divine attributes being eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And what the Hashwiyya replied — may God distance them! — that He speaks at times and is silent at other times according to His will, and when He is silent His speech does not cease, but rather He is silent and conceals His speech, i.e., He hides it — exalted is God above their saying, a great exaltation — this is nonsense that no believer nor rational person would be pleased to say. And whoever wishes to stand upon many of their scandals to thank God for safety and desire from Him the permanence of well-being — let him consult 'Sharh al-Aqida al-Kubra'.

This passage refutes the deviant sect known as al-Ḥashwiyya, who claimed that God sometimes speaks and sometimes is silent, and that silence does not negate speech but merely hides it. The author strongly condemns this view, calling it nonsense (hawas) that no believer or rational person would accept. He then directs the reader to a larger work (Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Kubrā) for more detailed exposure of their errors. The refutation relies on the earlier proof that speech is eternal and cannot be subject to change or cessation.

Refutation of the Qadariyya Doctrine of Direct and Generated Effects
Sanusi explains and refutes the Qadariyya claim of direct and generated causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qadariyya doctrine of direct and generated effects and the Ash'ari refutation based on the principle that created capacity has no causal influence in either case.

Core Idea
Created capacity has no causal effect—neither directly nor by generation.

Sanusi confronts a dangerous theological error: the Qadariyya claim that creatures truly produce actions, some directly and some by a process called generation (tawallud). He dismisses both claims with a simple but devastating observation—that in either case, the created capacity is equally powerless. The following passage explains the Qadariyya view and the Ash'ari counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's own statement
Read the opening phrase 'Neither by direct action nor by generation' and its explanation. Notice that Sanusi defines the terms and sets up the refutation.
The phrase 'lā mubāsharatan wa lā tawalludan' is a key summary of the Ash'ari position.
2
Learn the Qadariyya classification
Identify the Qadariyya claim of direct actions (e.g., hand movement) and generated actions (e.g., moving a stone). Understand their reasoning that generated effects are produced indirectly via a prior action.
They even mention the strength of nerves as a factor, showing their naturalistic bent.
3
Grasp the refutation
Recognize that Sanusi's refutation is implicit: since the created capacity has no real causal effect in either case, the Qadariyya distinction is irrelevant. Both types of actions are directly created by Allah.
This ties back to the earlier lesson on the lack of causal influence of originated capacities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال القول بتأثير ما سوى الله تعالى بالمباشرة والتولد. قوله: (لا مباشرةً ولا تولُّداً) يعني: أنَّه لا فرق في عدم تأثيرِ القدرةِ الحادثةِ بينَ الفعل الذي وُجِدَ معَها في محلّ واحدٍ؛ كحركة يد المختار مثلاً، وبين الفعل الذي يُوجَدُ خارجاً عن محلها؛ كحركة المفتاح والسيف مثلاً عند حركة اليد، وهو الذي يُعنى بالتولُّدِ، ونبَّهَ بهذا على مذهب القدرية مجوس هذهِ الأمَّةِ - أبعدَهم الله تعالى - حيث قالوا: إنَّ القدرة الحادثة والقوَّةَ التي خلق الله تعالى في الحيوانِ هي التي بها يخترع الحيوان أفعاله على وفق ما يريدُ. قالوا - أهلكَهمُ اللهُ تعالى -: فمنها ما يخترعه بها مباشرةً؛ أي: واسطة؛ وذلكَ كحركاتِ ذاتِهِ وسكناتها التي في محلّ قدرته وقوته، ومنها ما يخترعُهُ بها تولُّداً؛ أي: بواسطة؛ وذلك كتحريكه الحجرَ أوِ السيفَ، أو رميه بالسهم ونحوه، أو جَرْحِهِ لشخص ونحو ذلك، فإِنَّ هذهِ الأشياءَ قالوا - أبعدَهم الله تعالى -: العبد هو الذي يخترعُها بقوَّتِهِ، لكن لا مباشرةً، بل بواسطة اختراعه حركةً في يدِهِ، واعتماداً بها على ذلكَ، قالوا - أهلكهم الله تعالى -: ويختلفُ الأثر في ذلكَ بحَسَبِ اختلافِ قوَّةِ العصب وضعفِهِ. ولهذا كانَتْ حقيقةُ التولدِ عندَهم وجود حادث عن مقدور بالقدرةِ الحادثة، فحركة الحجرِ مثلاً مُتولّدةٌ عندَهم؛ لأنَّها حادث نشأ عن شيء مقدور بالقدرة الحادثة، وهو حركة اليد والاعتماد بها.
Refutation of the statement that other than God Most High has effect by direct action and generation. His statement: 'Neither by direct action nor by generation' means: that there is no difference in the lack of effect of the originated capacity between an act that occurs with it in the same place—like the movement of the hand of the chosen agent, for example—and an act that occurs outside its locus—like the movement of the key and sword, for example, upon the movement of the hand—and this is what is meant by generation. And he warned by this about the doctrine of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this nation—may God Most High distance them—for they said: 'Indeed, the originated capacity and the power that God Most High created in the animal are that by which the animal invents its actions according to what it wills.' They said—may God Most High destroy them—: 'Some of them it invents by direct action, i.e., without mediation, such as the movements and rests of its own self that are in the locus of its capacity and power. And some of them it invents by generation, i.e., by mediation, such as its moving the stone or the sword, or its shooting the arrow and the like, or its wounding a person and similar things.' For these things they said—may God Most High distance them—: 'The servant is the one who invents them by his power, but not directly; rather, by mediation of his invention of a movement in his hand and reliance upon that.' They said—may God Most High destroy them—: 'And the effect differs in that according to the difference in strength and weakness of the nerves.' And because of this, the reality of generation (tawallud) according to them is the existence of an event from something that is within the capacity of the originated capacity. So the movement of the stone, for example, is generated according to them because it is an event that arose from something that is within the capacity of the originated capacity, namely the movement of the hand and reliance upon it.
Source pages p. 369

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Qadariyya doctrine of direct and generated effects. It begins by explaining the author's own statement: 'Neither by direct action nor by generation' (lā mubāsharatan wa lā tawalludan). The meaning is that there is no distinction in the lack of causal efficacy of the created capacity (al-qudra al-hāditha) between an action occurring in the same locus as that capacity (e.g., the hand of the chosen agent) and an action occurring outside that locus (e.g., the movement of a key or sword when the hand moves). The latter is what is called 'generation' (tawallud). The author then uses this to warn against the Qadariyya, whom he disparagingly calls the Magians of this nation. They claim that the created capacity and power in animals enables them to invent their own actions according to their will. They further divide these actions: some are directly invented (e.g., movements of the body itself), and some are invented by generation (e.g., moving a stone via a hand movement, shooting an arrow, etc.). In the latter case, they claim the servant invents the effect through his power but indirectly, by first inventing a movement in his hand and relying on it. They also claim that the resulting effect varies based on the strength or weakness of the nerves. The author then states the Qadariyya definition of generation: an event arising from something that is within the capacity of the created capacity, such as the movement of a stone arising from the movement of the hand and reliance on it. The refutation is implicit: since all actions—whether direct or generated—are equally without causal influence from the created capacity, the Qadariyya position is false. True causality belongs to God alone.

Key Terms
مباشرة
تولد
قدرة حادثة
القدرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Generated effects (tawallud) are examples of secondary causality where the creature's power indirectly causes external events.
Correction: In Ash'ari theology, the created capacity has no causal influence whatsoever; both direct and generated effects are directly created by Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the core argument Sanusi uses to refute the Qadariyya doctrine of generated (tawallud) effects?
A) Generated effects have no physical basis.
B) There is no difference in the lack of causal influence between direct and generated actions.
C) The Qadariyya are polytheists.
D) The created capacity only works by direct contact.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation of the Qadariyya doctrine rests on the principle that created capacity has no causal effect, whether the action is direct or generated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often feel that your own strength is the cause of your successes. After reading this lesson, how do you reconcile that feeling with the truth that all effects are from God alone? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that you do not cause anything—even indirectly? Can you identify one area where you trust your own power more than God's?
?
The Qadariyya claimed that humans invent their actions by their power. Do you ever act as if you are the originator of your deeds, forgetting that God creates both the action and its effect? How can you remind yourself of your complete dependence on God in every moment?
?
If you truly accept that created capacity has no causal effect, how does that affect your view of your own abilities and achievements? Is your sense of pride diminished or deepened? Will you today thank God for even the smallest movement of your hand, acknowledging Him as its Creator?

The Sunni Middle Path on Human Power
Sanusi states the Sunni position on human power and distinguishes it from Jabriyya and Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position on temporal human power (qudra hāditha) and how it avoids both Jabriyya's denial of human capacity and Qadariyya's claim that humans create their own acts.

Core Idea
Sunnis affirm human power but deny it any causal effect; acts belong to Allah alone.

Sanusi presents the Sunni position on human action by staking out a middle ground between two extreme sects. The Jabriyya deny any temporal power in the servant, making all acts involuntary. The Qadariyya claim the servant creates his own acts through that power, deserving reward and punishment. Sunnis affirm a temporal power that accompanies the act without influencing it, preserving both human responsibility and divine sovereignty. This passage explains that distinction and clarifies that reward and punishment are conventional signs, not rational necessities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two key Sunni claims
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Look for two statements: one affirming a temporal power with the act, and another denying that this power has any effect on the act.
The claims are introduced by 'إنَّ مع الفعل' and 'ليس لتلك القدرة'.
2
See how the first claim separates from Jabriyya
The first claim affirms a temporal power in the servant. The Jabriyya completely deny this, so the Sunni position is opposed to them.
Jabriyya equate all acts as involuntary; Sunnis distinguish voluntary acts.
3
See how the second claim separates from Qadariyya
The second claim denies that the power has any effect. The Qadariyya assert that the servant creates his acts through this power, so the Sunni position denies that causation.
The Qadariyya are called 'Magians of this ummah' for their dualism.
4
Understand the nature of reward and punishment
Sanusi reiterates that reward and punishment are not rational consequences of acts but conventional signs set by Allah. This supports the denial of human causal power.
This prevents the conclusion that humans deserve reward/punishment by their own power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا جبرية ولا قدريَّة في مذهب أهل السنة ] فخرج لكَ مِنْ هذا : أنَّ بقولنا : ( إِنَّ مَعَ الفعل الذي لم يُحِسَّ صاحبُهُ فيهِ الاضطرار قدرةً حادثة في العبدِ هي عَرَضُ مِنَ الأَعراضِ ؛ كالعلم ونحوه ، تتعلق بالفعل وإنْ لم نرَ لها تأثيراً فيهِ أصلاً ) .. انفصلنا عن مذهب الجبريَّةِ القائلين بنفي قدرةٍ حادثة في العبدِ مطلقاً وبقولنا : ( ليس لتلك القدرة الحادثة تأثير في الفعل أصلاً ، وإِنَّما تتعلَّقُ بهِ وتصاحبه فقط ) .. انفصلنا مذهب القدريَّةِ مجوس هذهِ الأمَّةِ القائلين بأنَّ تلك القدرة الحادثة في العبدِ بها يخترع العبدُ أفعاله على حسَبِ إرادتِهِ ، قالوا : وبذلك أطاع وعصى ، وعليهِ أُثيب وعُوقبَ ! وقد سبقَ لكَ : أنَّ الثواب والعقاب لا سبب لهما عقلاً عند أهل الحقِّ (۱) ، وإنَّما الطاعات والمعاصي أمارات جَعْليَّةٌ ، لا علل عقليَّةٌ فتحقق بهذا : تمييز المذهب الحقِّ عن المذهبينِ الفاسدين ؛ وهما مذهبا الجبرية والقدريَّةِ ؛ فإنَّ تمييزه عنهما مما يلتبس على كثيرٍ . ما ذكره واضح ، وحاصله : أنَّ المذاهب المعروفة المشهورة في الفعلِ الذي لم يُحِسَّ فيهِ صاحبُهُ الاضطرار . . ثلاثة : واحد منها حق واثنان فاسدانِ [ بدعة مذهب الجبريَّة ] أحدهما : مذهب الجبريَّةِ القائلين بنفي القدرة الحادثة مطلقاً وذهبوا إلى التسوية بينَ الأفعالِ الاختياريَّةِ والأفعال الاضطرارية ، : (۱) تقدم ( ص ۳۷۱ ) .
[Neither Jabriyya nor Qadariyya in the school of Ahl al-Sunnah] So from this it follows for you: that by our saying, 'Indeed, with the act in which its agent does not feel compulsion there is a temporal power (qudra hāditha) in the servant, which is an accident (ʿaraḍ) among accidents, like knowledge and the like, that is connected to the act even if we see no effect for it in it at all' – we have separated ourselves from the school of the Jabriyya, who affirm no temporal power in the servant absolutely. And by our saying, 'That temporal power has no effect in the act at all, rather it is only connected to it and accompanies it' – we have separated ourselves from the school of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this ummah, who say that by that temporal power in the servant, the servant invents his actions according to his will; they said: thereby he obeys and disobeys, and upon that he is rewarded and punished. And it has preceded for you that reward and punishment have no rational cause according to Ahl al-Ḥaqq (1), rather acts of obedience and disobedience are conventional indicators (amārāt jaʿliyya), not rational causes. So realize by this the distinction of the true school from the two corrupt schools, namely the schools of the Jabriyya and the Qadariyya; for distinguishing it from them is something that confuses many. What he mentioned is clear, and its summary is that the well-known, famous schools concerning the act in which its agent does not feel compulsion are three: one is true and two are corrupt. [Innovation of the school of the Jabriyya] One of them: the school of the Jabriyya, who affirm the negation of temporal power absolutely, and they went to equating voluntary acts and involuntary acts. (1) Preceded (p. 371).
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of two deviant sects concerning human action. The author first presents the Sunni position in two parts: (1) affirming a temporal power (qudra hāditha) in the servant, which is an accident like knowledge, that accompanies the voluntary act without any causal effect; (2) denying that this power has any influence (ta'thīr) on the act – it merely connects to it. The first part distinguishes Sunni from Jabriyya, who completely deny any such temporal power. The second part distinguishes Sunni from Qadariyya, who claim that the servant creates his own acts through this power and thus deserves reward and punishment. The author then rebukes the Qadariyya as 'the Magians of this ummah' and reiterates that reward and punishment have no rational cause; rather, acts are merely conventional signs (amārāt jaʿliyya) that Allah uses to assign reward and punishment, not rational causes. He concludes that the true madhhab (Ahl al-Sunnah) is now clearly distinguished from the two false ones – Jabriyya and Qadariyya – noting that this distinction confuses many. Finally, he summarizes that there are three schools on this issue: one correct (Sunni) and two corrupt. He then begins describing the first corrupt school, Jabriyya, who completely deny temporal power and equate all acts (voluntary and involuntary), but the text ends there.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Affirming a temporal power in the servant commits one to Qadariyya's belief that humans create their own acts.
Correction: Sunnis affirm temporal power but explicitly deny it has any causal effect; the act is wholly created by Allah. The power is merely concomitant, not creative.

Checkpoint
How does the Sunni position on temporal power (qudra hāditha) differ from the Qadariyya position?
A) Sunnis deny any power in the servant; Qadariyya affirm it.
B) Sunnis affirm power but deny it has any effect; Qadariyya affirm it creates the act.
C) Both affirm power creates the act, but Sunnis say God creates it.
D) Sunnis say power is eternal; Qadariyya say it is temporal.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position on human power is a middle path: affirming temporal capacity without causal effect, distinguishing it from Jabriyya's denial and Qadariyya's creationism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you sin, do you unconsciously excuse yourself by feeling that you 'couldn't help it' (Jabriyya tendency) or do you blame yourself as if you created that sin (Qadariyya tendency)? Which tendency is more dangerous for your faith, and how can the Sunni middle path correct it?
?
The passage says reward and punishment are conventional signs, not rational causes. If you knew that your obedience does not 'deserve' reward by itself, would you still obey? What does your answer reveal about the sincerity of your intention?

Refutation of the Jabriyya: Distinction of Actions and Basis of Obligation
Sanusi refutes the Jabriyya on two counts with rational and scriptural arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key errors of the Jabriyya: their failure to distinguish voluntary from involuntary actions based on the presence of originated power, and their denial of the locus of obligation based on usual capacity.

Core Idea
The Jabriyya confuse metaphysical causality with legal responsibility.

The Jabriyya (extreme determinists) claimed that all human actions are directly from God with no distinction. This leads to two grave errors: they ignore the rational distinction between voluntary and involuntary acts, and they undermine the very basis of religious obligation. In this passage, Sanusi refutes both errors using reason and scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Get a sense of the overall argument: two refutations of the Jabriyya.
The author uses strong language: 'sukhafa' al-'uqul' (weak of intellect) and 'mubtadi'a' (innovators).
2
Identify the two refutations
The passage has two main parts: first, the Jabriyya are intellectually weak for missing the distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions; second, they are innovators for denying the locus of obligation (mahall al-taklif). Notice how each is argued.
3
Examine the distinction between actions
According to Sanusi, the only rational difference between voluntary and involuntary actions is that voluntary acts are accompanied by an originated power (qudra ḥāditha) in the slave, while involuntary acts are not. Both are directly created by Allah.
This distinction avoids attributing real causal power to the slave while preserving the basis for moral responsibility.
4
Examine the basis of obligation
The second error: the Jabriyya deny that there is a locus for obligation and reward/punishment. Sanusi argues that taklif in the Law is based on usual capacity (wus' 'ādi), not on effective power. The Quranic verse 'Allah does not charge a soul except with its capacity' supports this.
Even though definitive proof shows no effect from the slave, ordinary capacity is sufficient for obligation.
5
Reflect on the overall point
Consider how both errors stem from conflating metaphysical causality (all actions from Allah) with legal responsibility (based on capacity). The passage guards against a simplistic determinism that destroys morality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أنهم سخفاء العقول من حيث إنهم خفي عليهم الفرق بينهما الذي شهدت به ضرورة العقول ودل بعد السبر التام على أنه لا فرق بينهما إلا كون الأولى الاختيارية مقارنة لقدرة حادثة بخلاف الثانية الاضطرارية وهم مبتدعة أيضا من حيث إنهم نفوا محل التكليف وأمارة الثواب والعقاب شرعا إذ التكليف إنما وقع في الشرع حسب اختياره تعالى بما هو مقدور المكلف وفي وسعه عادة والبرهان القطعي وإن كان قد قام بأنه لا أثر للعبد في فعل من الأفعال ألبتة وأن الاختيارية والاضطرارية سواء في أنهما فعل الله تعالى بلا واسطة وأن العبد لا أثر له فيهما ألبتة ولا في أثر ما عموما فإنه يكفي في صدق ما أشار إليه القرآن بقوله لا يكلف الله نفسا إلا وسعها حصول الوسع العادي فيما كلف به مولانا جل وعز
And there is no doubt that they are weak of intellect, in that the distinction between them (voluntary and involuntary) was hidden from them, which the necessity of intellects witnessed, and after thorough investigation it indicated that there is no difference between them except that the first, the voluntary, is accompanied by an originated power, unlike the second, the involuntary. And they are also innovators in that they denied the locus of obligation and the sign of reward and punishment in the Law; for obligation in the Law occurs only according to His choice, Exalted, as what is within the capability of the obligated person and his usual capacity. And even though definitive proof has established that the slave has absolutely no effect in any act at all, and that the voluntary and involuntary are equal in that they are both the act of God without mediation, and that the slave has no effect in them at all, nor in any effect whatsoever generally, yet it is sufficient for the truth of what the Quran indicated in His saying 'God does not charge a soul except with its capacity' the existence of usual capacity concerning that with which our Master, Great and Mighty, obligated.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage refutes the Jabriyya (determinists) on two counts. First, the author states that they are intellectually feeble because they fail to see the clear distinction between voluntary and involuntary actions. Reason itself ('ضرورة العقول') confirms this distinction: voluntary acts are accompanied by an originated power (قدرة حادثة) in the slave, while involuntary acts are not. Second, the Jabriyya are innovators because they deny the basis of religious obligation (محل التكليف) and the sign of reward and punishment. Obligation in the Law is based on what is within the slave's usual capacity (وسع عادي), as supported by the Quranic verse (2:286). Even if reason proves that the slave has no real effect—since all acts are directly from God—still the criterion of obligation is ordinary capacity, not effective power. Thus, the Jabriyya err by conflating metaphysical causality with the basis of legal responsibility.

اختيارية
اضطرارية
قدرة حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Jabriyya are correct that all actions are from God and thus no distinction is needed, and that obligation is meaningless.
Correction: The passage shows that reason distinguishes voluntary acts by presence of originated power, and that legal obligation is based on usual capacity, not causal effectiveness.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the only difference between voluntary and involuntary actions?
A) The voluntary is good and the involuntary is evil.
B) The voluntary is accompanied by an originated power (qudra ḥāditha) in the slave, the involuntary is not.
C) The voluntary is caused by the slave, the involuntary by God.
D) The voluntary is within the slave's capacity, the involuntary is not.

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya are refuted for both ignoring the rational distinction between actions and for denying the basis of obligation, which rests on usual capacity not effective power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you secretly rationalize that 'God willed it, so it wasn't really my fault'? After reading this lesson, what practical step will you take to embrace your responsibility for your voluntary actions, even when you feel powerless?
?
You often tell yourself, 'I can't help it; that's just the way I am.' But this lesson says God holds you accountable based on your usual capacity, not your excuses. Which habit or sin are you currently blaming on circumstances, and how will you instead see it as a voluntary act requiring repentance?
?
The Jabriyya denied the very foundation of obligation. Have you ever ignored a religious duty because you felt it was 'too hard' or 'beyond your ability'? How will you respond to the verse 'God does not charge a soul except with its capacity' when you are tempted to give up on a command?
